reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig

class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits

model_name = "google/electra-small-discriminator"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 32
batch_size = 1024

def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

In `transformers`, `PreTrainedModel.init_weights` calls `self.apply(self._init_weights)`. `_init_weights` for `Electra` uses `normal_(mean=0.0, std=self.config.initializer_range)`. The random values depend on the RNG state. Then `get_q(row, head, dim) = qkv[row * 3H + head * head_dim + dim]` `get_k(row, head, dim) = qkv[row * 3H + H + head * head_dim + dim]` `get_v(row, head, dim) = qkv[row * 3H + 2*H + head * head_dim + dim]` This is fine. Let's refine the fused attention kernel. **Fused Attention Kernel** ```cuda __global__ void fused_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ qkv, const float* __restrict__ o_w, const float* __restrict__ o_b, const float* __restrict__ residual, const float* __restrict__ ln_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_b, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int S, int H, int num_heads, int head_dim) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; int warp = tid / 32; const float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); const float* qkv_b = qkv + b * S * 3 * H; const float* res_b = residual + b * S * H; float* out_b = out + b * S * H; // Shared memory: store attention output [S, H] or per-head then concat. // We also need Q, K for a head to compute scores. // Max SMEM usage: // scores [S, S] for one head? 32*32*4 = 4KB. // q_tile [S, head_dim] = 32*64*4 = 8KB. // k_tile [S, head_dim] = 8KB. // v_tile [S, head_dim] = 8KB. // attn_out [S, head_dim] = 8KB. // Total ~36KB. Very comfortable. __shared__ float smem_scores[32 * 32]; // or dynamic __shared__ float smem_q[32 * 64]; __shared__ float smem_k[32 * 64]; __shared__ float smem_v[32 * 64]; __shared__ float smem_attn_out[32 * 64]; // per head output // We'll process 4 heads one by one. for (int h = 0; h < num_heads; ++h) { // Load Q, K, V for this head into SMEM // We have S*head_dim elements. 1024 elements per head. // 256 threads -> each loads 4 elements. for (int idx = tid; idx < S * head_dim; idx += blockDim.x) { int row = idx / head_dim; int col = idx % head_dim; int q_offset = row * 3 * H + h * head_dim + col; int k_offset = row * 3 * H + H + h * head_dim + col; int v_offset = row * 3 * H + 2 * H + h * head_dim + col; smem_q[idx] = qkv_b[q_offset]; smem_k[idx] = qkv_b[k_offset]; smem_v[idx] = qkv_b[v_offset]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Q @ K^T -> scores [S, S] // scores[i][j] = dot(Q[i], K[j]) // 256 threads. // S*S = 1024. // Each thread computes 4 score elements. // But we need to be careful about __syncthreads. // We can compute in registers without storing all scores, but we need all scores for softmax row. // S=32, so we can store in SMEM. for (int idx = tid; idx < S * S; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / S; int j = idx % S; float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { dot += smem_q[i * head_dim + d] * smem_k[j * head_dim + d]; } smem_scores[idx] = dot * scale; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax per row (over S=32) // 32 warps? We have 8 warps (256 threads). // One warp per row? But we have 32 rows and 8 warps. Not enough. // We can have each thread process one row? 256 threads, 32 rows. // 8 threads per row. Use warp shuffle for reduction. // But rows might cross warp boundaries. // Let's just have each thread compute one row if S <= 32 and blockDim.x >= 32. // Actually, rows 0..31. We have 256 threads. So 8 threads per row. // Let's process rows in groups. for (int row = warp; row < S; row += 8) { // 8 warps // threads in warp process one row // Each thread gets some columns? But we need max and sum over all columns. // Since S=32 and warp size=32, we can have threads in a warp handle columns. // Wait, each warp has 32 threads. Perfect for 32 columns. // So assign one row per warp. // But we have 8 warps and 32 rows. So each warp handles 4 rows? No, warp index. // Simpler: use the first 32 threads (one warp) to handle one row at a time, iteratively for 32 rows. // But that serializes softmax over rows. // Or, since blockDim.x = 256, we can process 8 rows in parallel (one per warp). // Each warp handles row = warp_id + 8*k. // warp 0 handles rows 0, 8, 16, 24. // warp 1 handles rows 1, 9, 17, 25. // etc. int row_idx = warp + (tid / 256) ... no. // Better: we don't need warp-level. S=32 is tiny. // We can compute softmax for all rows using 32 threads sequentially. // But 32 threads might be slow. // Let's do: thread `r` computes softmax for row `r` (r < S), but we only have 32 rows. // But blockDim.x = 256. So threads 0..31 handle rows 0..31. Threads 32..255 idle for softmax. // Then for PV, all 256 threads work. // This is fine because softmax is tiny. } // Actually, let's simplify: // Use 256 threads. First 1024 elements for scores is handled above. // Now softmax: 32 rows. // Each row: find max, subtract, exp, sum. // We can do this with 32 threads (one warp) doing one row at a time in a loop of 32 iterations. // But maybe faster: thread `r` (0..31) handles row `r`. 32 threads. // We have many threads, so we can use 32 threads for softmax and the rest wait. // But then PV matmul uses all threads. It's okay. // Or: use 256 threads for softmax by having each thread handle a row for a subset of cols, and then warp reduce. // Since S=32 < warp_size, it's easier to have one thread per row. // Let's just have the first 32 threads compute softmax for 32 rows. if (tid < S) { int row = tid; float max_val = -1e9f; for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = smem_scores[row * S + j]; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { float val = expf(smem_scores[row * S + j] - max_val); smem_scores[row * S + j] = val; sum += val; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum; for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { smem_scores[row * S + j] *= inv_sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute scores @ V -> attn_out [S, head_dim] // 256 threads. S*head_dim = 2048 elements per head. Wait, S=32, head_dim=64, so 2048 elements. // 256 threads -> each computes 8 elements. for (int idx = tid; idx < S * head_dim; idx += blockDim.x) { int i = idx / head_dim; int d = idx % head_dim; float val = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < S; ++j) { val += smem_scores[i * S + j] * smem_v[j * head_dim + d]; } smem_attn_out[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Write concatenated head output to a temporary global buffer or directly to O_proj // We can do O_proj here. // But O_proj is large. Let's store concatenated output in SMEM? We have 32*256 = 32KB for full attn out. // But we are processing head by head. We can accumulate O_proj output in registers and write to global mem after all heads. } // Wait, accumulating O_proj output across heads requires storing the result somewhere. // Option: after each head, we compute O_proj contribution for that head and add to output. // O_w is [H, H]. For head h, the input is [S, head_dim] and it contributes to [S, H]. // Specifically, O_w maps from H to H. The output for token s, dim d is: // out[s, d] += dot(attn_out[s, :], O_w[:, d]) // But attn_out is only for head h: [S, head_dim]. Wait, standard O_proj is over concatenated heads. // So we need the full [S, H] vector to multiply with O_w. // Thus we should store all heads in SMEM first. // Change plan: use a larger SMEM for full attention output [S, H] = 32 * 256 * 4 = 32KB. // And per-head intermediate [S, head_dim] = 8KB. // Total SMEM: scores 4KB + q 8KB + k 8KB + v 8KB + attn_head 8KB + full_attn 32KB = 68KB. Still okay (< 100KB). // However, we need O_w and O_b and residual and LN params too. But they are global reads. // Let's maintain `smem_full_attn` [S, H] = 32*256 floats. // Initialize to 0. // After computing each head's output in `smem_attn_out`, copy it to `smem_full_attn` at the right offset. // Let's redefine SMEM as dynamic or static arrays. } ``` This is getting quite involved but manageable. Here's a revised plan: - Custom kernel 1: Embeddings + LayerNorm - Per layer: - cuBLAS: QKV projection [32768, 256] @ [256, 768] - Custom kernel 2: Attention (softmax + V attention) for all heads - cuBLAS: Output projection [32768, 256] @ [256, 256] - Custom kernel 3: Residual add + LayerNorm - cuBLAS: MLP expansion [32768, 256] @ [256, 1024] - Custom kernel 4: GELU + bias add - cuBLAS: MLP contraction [32768, 1024] @ [1024, 256] - Custom kernel 5: Residual add + LayerNorm - cuBLAS: Final head [32768, 256] @ [256, 1] That's 6 per layer * 12 + 2 = 74 kernel launches. Still a lot. Can I reduce this? Yes, by fusing more: - Custom kernel 3 + 5 can just be one `add_layernorm` kernel - Custom kernel 4 can be `gelu_add` kernel So per layer: 5 launches. Total: 5*12 + 2 = 62. What if I fuse the residual adds into the layernorm kernel? That's already what I'm doing. Can I fuse QKV projection + attention into one custom kernel? For the projection part, I'd be doing a [256, 768] matmul for each token. With 32768 tokens, and each block handling a few tokens, I could load the weights into shared memory and compute the projection there. Per token, QKV projection is 256*768 = 196K multiply-adds. At 67 TFLOPS, that's about 3 microseconds per token. For 32 tokens in a block, that's 96 microseconds. This is reasonable to do in a kernel without cuBLAS, especially if we can use shared memory for the weights. For 256 hidden -> 768 (QKV), the weight matrix is 256*768*4 = 786KB. That's too big for shared memory (max 227KB). We could tile it: process 256 -> 256 for Q, then 256 -> 256 for K, then 256 -> 256 for V. Each 256->256 weight matrix is 256KB, still too big for shared memory if we want other buffers too. 256*256*4 = 262KB > 227KB max shared memory. So we can't even fit one projection matrix in shared memory. What if we tile smaller? Say 128 -> 256: 128*256*4 = 128KB. That fits! Then we process in 2 tiles for each projection. This is getting quite complicated. Without tensor core support in custom kernels (hard to implement in raw CUDA), manual matmul in shared memory might not beat cuBLAS anyway. OK here's what I'll do. I'll use `at::matmul` in C++ for all linear projections (which calls optimized cuBLAS). For the elementwise and attention parts, I'll write custom CUDA kernels. I'll also fuse what I can. Actually, let me think about whether I can just use PyTorch's F.linear in C++. Yes, `at::linear` does matmul + bias under the hood. Wait, for maximum efficiency, I can use `torch::addmm` which is the batched matrix multiply with bias addition. This is highly optimized. OK, let me design the actual C++ code: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cuda_fp16.h> #include <math.h> // Kernel: embeddings + layernorm __global__ void embeddings_layernorm_kernel( const int* input_ids, const float* word_embed, const float* pos_embed, const float* type_embed, const float* ln_weight, const float* ln_bias, float* output, int batch_size, int seq_len, int hidden_size, int vocab_size ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch_size * seq_len; if (idx >= total) return; int b = idx / seq_len; int s = idx % seq_len; int token_id = input_ids[idx]; for (int h = threadIdx.y; h < hidden_size; h += blockDim.y) { float val = word_embed[token_id * hidden_size + h] + pos_embed[s * hidden_size + h] + type_embed[h]; // token_type_ids usually 0 // Store temporarily, then do layernorm // ... } } ``` Hmm, the blockDim.y approach might not be the best. For layernorm, we typically want all threads in a block to cooperate on one token (or a few tokens) since they need to compute mean and variance collectively. For seq_len=32, batch=1024, total tokens = 32768. If each block handles 1 token and uses 256 threads (one per hidden element), we need 32768 blocks. With 256 threads per block, that's 256 threads * 32768 blocks = 8,388,608 total thread slots. Since H100 has 132 SMs and each can run up to 2048 threads, max concurrent = 270,336. So we'd need about 31 waves. That's a lot of waves! Actually, most kernels with this many blocks will run fine, but it might not fully utilize the GPU for small operations. What if each block handles 4 tokens with 64 threads each? Or 8 tokens with 32 threads each? We need enough threads per token to compute LayerNorm efficiently. For hidden_size=256, 256 threads per token is ideal for layernorm. But that only gives 32 tokens per block if we use 8192 threads, which is too many. CUDA blocks are limited to 1024 threads. So we could have: - 256 threads per token - 4 tokens per block = 1024 threads - Total blocks = 32768 / 4 = 8192 blocks 8192 blocks / 132 SMs = ~62 blocks per SM. That's great! If each block's shared memory usage is small, we can have good occupancy. For layernorm on 4 tokens with 256 hidden: - Need temporary storage for each token: 256 floats = 1KB - 4 tokens = 4KB shared memory - Plus other negligible overhead With 4KB shared memory, we could have many concurrent blocks per SM. This seems like a good configuration. OK, I think I can make this work. Let me design the kernels more carefully. **Kernel 1: Embeddings + LayerNorm** - Block: (256, 4) - actually CUDA blocks are 1D or 2D/3D. Let me use 1D with 1024 threads, and each thread handles one hidden element of one token. Or use 2D with (256, 4). - Actually for reduction (mean/variance), all threads working on the same token need to communicate. So 2D block with (256, 4) means 256 threads per token, 4 tokens. We can use a 2D grid. Let me think about the reduction. For layernorm, each token needs: 1. Sum over hidden dim 2. Sum of squares over hidden dim 3. Normalize 4. Scale and shift With 256 threads per token, we can do parallel reduction in shared memory. **Kernel 2: Attention** - Input Q, K, V: [batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim] - Output: [batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim] - Block per sequence or per head For batch=1024, num_heads=4, seq_len=32, head_dim=64: - With 1 block per sequence, 1024 blocks - Each block handles all 4 heads for its sequence - 128 threads per block: process all 4 heads Attention steps: 1. For each head h: - For each query position i (0..31): - Compute dot products Q[i]·K[j] for all j (0..31) - Softmax - Compute output = sum_j softmax_ij * V[j] For 32x32 attention, each query position needs 32 dot products of size 64, plus softmax of 32 elements, plus 32 dot products of size 64 with V. This is very small. We can have each warp handle one (head, query_position) pair: - 4 heads * 32 positions = 128 warps - With 1 block per sequence, we need 128 warps = 4096 threads per block! Too many. What if each block handles 1 head for 1 sequence? - 32 query positions, each handled by one warp (32 threads) - Warp computes Q[i] @ K^T (32 elements) - Then does softmax on 32 elements - Then computes attention @ V for that position - Total: 32 warps = 1024 threads per block - This is exactly the block size limit! And we'd need 1024 sequences * 4 heads = 4096 blocks. With 1024 threads per block, we'd get maybe 1 block per SM (depending on occupancy), so 132 concurrent blocks, processing 132 heads at a time. Total 4096 blocks / 132 = ~31 waves. Actually, that's a lot of waves. The attention is so small that most of the time is memory access, not compute. What if we process all 4 heads in one block with fewer threads? Say 256 threads = 8 warps. Each warp handles 4 query positions (8 threads cooperate on one position). Or each warp handles one head with 8 query positions each. Total: 4 heads * 8 positions = 32 work items. With 8 warps, that's 4 work items per warp. Each work item is one query position's attention. Hmm, this is getting complex. Let me think about whether attention is even a bottleneck. For seq_len=32, num_heads=4, head_dim=64: - Q@K^T: 1024 sequences * 4 heads * 32 queries * (32 keys * 64 mults) = 268M FLOPs - Softmax: negligible compute - Attention@V: same as Q@K^T ≈ 268M FLOPs - Total attention FLOPs: ~536M Compare to QKV projection: [32768, 256] @ [256, 768] = 32768 * 256 * 768 * 2 = 12.9 GFLOPs Attention is about 20x less compute than the projections! So the projections dominate. For the whole model: - QKV + output proj per layer: ~17 GFLOPs - MLP per layer: ~17 GFLOPs - Attention per layer: ~0.5 GFLOPs - Total per layer: ~34.5 GFLOPs - 12 layers: ~414 GFLOPs - Final head: negligible So attention is only about 1.5% of total compute. Even if I write a terrible custom attention kernel, it won't matter much for overall speed. This simplifies things! I can write a simple attention kernel and focus on optimizing the rest. What dominates is the MLP and projection matmuls. For those, `at::matmul` in C++ (which calls cuBLAS) is probably optimal. So the main opportunities for speedup are: 1. Since it's small, I'll write a simple one: ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* qkv, // [batch, seq_len, 3*hidden] or separate Q, K, V float* output, // [batch, seq_len, hidden] int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_heads, int head_dim, int hidden_size ) { // Each block handles one sequence // Each warp handles one head // Each thread in warp handles one query position int seq_idx = blockIdx.x; int head_idx = threadIdx.y; int q_pos = threadIdx.x; // 0 to seq_len-1 if (seq_idx >= batch_size || head_idx >= num_heads || q_pos >= seq_len) return; const float* q = qkv + seq_idx * seq_len * 3 * hidden_size + q_pos * 3 * hidden_size + head_idx * head_dim; const float* k_base = q + hidden_size; // K starts after Q const float* v_base = q + 2 * hidden_size; // V starts after K // Compute Q*K^T float scores[32]; // seq_len max float max_score = -1e9f; for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < seq_len; k_pos++) { float dot = 0; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q[d] * k_base[k_pos * 3 * hidden_size + d]; } scores[k_pos] = dot / sqrtf(head_dim); max_score = fmaxf(max_score, scores[k_pos]); } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0; for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < seq_len; k_pos++) { scores[k_pos] = expf(scores[k_pos] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[k_pos]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < seq_len; k_pos++) { scores[k_pos] *= inv_sum; } // Compute attention @ V float out[64]; // head_dim max for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out[d] = 0; for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < seq_len; k_pos++) { out[d] += scores[k_pos] * v_base[k_pos * 3 * hidden_size + d]; } } // Write output float* out_ptr = output + seq_idx * seq_len * hidden_size + q_pos * hidden_size + head_idx * head_dim; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out_ptr[d] = out[d]; } } ``` Wait, this is going to be very slow because: 1. Every thread is doing a loop over head_dim (64) and seq_len (32), with memory access patterns that might not be coalesced. 2. There's no shared memory usage at all. 3. Each warp has 32 threads doing independent work, which means divergent execution (each thread accesses different memory). Actually, since each thread handles one query position in one head, and there are 32 threads in a warp, the warp handles 32 query positions in one head. Wait no, if `threadIdx.x` is q_pos and goes from 0 to 31, then the warp handles all 32 query positions! But they all access different Q values, causing divergence? No, accessing different memory addresses within a warp is fine on modern GPUs - it just means more memory transactions. This is 32 * 32 * 64 = 65536 multiply-adds per warp. At 67 TFLOPS on H100, a warp can do maybe 2-4 TFLOPS, so 65536 operations takes... very little time. The bottleneck is memory bandwidth. Here's my refined architecture plan: 1. **C++ host function**: electra_forward_cuda - Takes input_ids and all weights - Handles the entire forward pass - Uses `at::matmul` for linear layers - Launches custom CUDA kernels for fusion 2. **Kernel 1**: `embeddings_and_layernorm` - Input: input_ids [batch, seq_len] - Weights: word_embed, pos_embed, type_embed, ln_gamma, ln_beta - Output: embeddings_layernorm [batch, seq_len, hidden] 3. **Per layer in C++ for loop**: a. `at::addmm` for QKV projection with bias b. **Kernel 2**: `attention_forward` - splits QKV, computes attention, writes context c. `at::addmm` for output projection with bias d. **Kernel 3**: `fused_add_layernorm` - adds residual, applies layernorm e. `at::addmm` for MLP expansion with bias f. **Kernel 4**: `fused_gelu` - applies GELU activation (with bias already added in addmm) g. `at::addmm` for MLP contraction with bias h. **Kernel 5**: `fused_add_layernorm` - adds residual, applies layernorm 4. **Final head**: a. `at::addmm` for dense(256->256) + GELU + dense(256->1 or 30522) Or use a custom kernel for the final bias adds if needed. Actually, I just realized that for layer norm, the standard formula is: ``` y = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * gamma + beta ``` If I already have the bias added before layer norm, then: ``` residual = x + bias y = layernorm(residual) ``` In many transformer implementations, the pattern is: ``` attention_output = self_attention(hidden_states) attention_output = dense(attention_output) + hidden_states attention_output = layer_norm(attention_output) ``` So in PyTorch: ```python attn_output = self_attention(hidden_states) attn_output = self.output.dense(attn_output) attn_output = self.output.dropout(attn_output) attn_output = attn_output + hidden_states attn_output = self.output.LayerNorm(attn_output) ``` The standard order is: dense -> dropout -> add residual -> layernorm. For inference, dropout is identity. So it becomes: dense -> add residual -> layernorm. Similarly for the MLP: dense1 -> activation -> dense2 -> dropout -> add residual -> layernorm. So yes, I can fuse: add residual + layernorm. For the attention block: QKV projection -> attention -> output projection -> add residual -> layernorm. For the FFN block: expansion -> GELU -> contraction -> add residual -> layernorm. Now, can I also fuse bias addition into the matmul? Yes, `at::addmm` does `output = bias + input @ weight.T` or `output = beta * input + alpha * (mat1 @ mat2)`. Actually, in PyTorch: ```python at::addmm(bias, input, weight.t()) ``` computes `bias + input @ weight.t()`, which is exactly linear(x) if weight is [out_feat, in_feat]. So I can use `at::addmm` for all linear layers and it will efficiently fuse bias + matmul in cuBLAS. Great! So the main custom kernels I need are: 1. `embeddings_and_layernorm` 2. `attention_forward` 3. `add_layernorm` 4. `gelu_forward` (if not fusing with contraction) Can I fuse GELU? PyTorch's GELU implementation might already be efficient, but having a custom kernel for it is nice. Actually, I can write a `gelu_bias` kernel that does `gelu(x + bias)` or since `at::addmm` already adds bias, it's just `gelu(x)`. Wait no, `at::addmm` does add the bias. So after `at::addmm` for MLP expansion, I just need GELU. PyTorch's `at::gelu` will launch one kernel. If I write my own, it's one kernel too. The win is small unless I can fuse GELU with the next contraction... but the next operation is another `at::addmm`, so I can't easily fuse them without doing manual matmul. So maybe I don't even need a custom kernel for GELU. The main wins are: 1. The embedding + layernorm fusion 2. The attention computation (fusing the elementwise attention ops) 3. The add + layernorm fusion 4. Avoiding Python overhead If I write 3 custom kernels and do the rest in C++ with `at::addmm`, I should get a decent speedup. But 12 layers * (1 QKV addmm + attention kernel + 1 output addmm + 1 add_layernorm + 1 expansion addmm + gelu + 1 contraction addmm + 1 add_layernorm) + 1 embedding kernel + 1 final addmm That's 12 * 8 + 2 = 98 CUDA kernels launched from C++. Compared to the reference which might launch ~120 from Python. The Python overhead savings might be modest. Hmm, but in C++ I can also reuse the same intermediate tensor buffers without reallocating, which saves memory allocation overhead. What about a more aggressive approach: a single custom kernel per layer that does attention? Actually, if attention compute is negligible, and matmuls dominate, then the only meaningful optimization is to make the matmuls faster. But `at::addmm` already calls the fastest cuBLAS. So what else can I do? One thing: for small matrices, cuBLAS might have significant launch overhead. Custom kernels that do small matmuls could be faster? No, cuBLAS is highly optimized even for small sizes. Wait, another idea. What if I use **CUTLASS** or **cuBLASLt** for the matmuls? Those support automatic fusion of add + matmul, and might have better epilogue fusion. But `at::addmm` already uses cuBLAS which is very good. What if I can **persist intermediate activations in shared memory or registers** across multiple operations? For example, after computing QKV projection, do attention entirely in shared memory without going to global memory. Then write only the output projection result. This would save memory bandwidth! For one layer: - Standard: QKV proj writes 32768 * 768 * 4 = 96MB - Attention reads 96MB, writes 33.5MB - Output proj reads 33.5MB, writes 33.5MB (with bias add, it's partially fused) - Add + LN reads/writes ~67MB - MLP expansion reads/writes ~134MB - GELU reads/writes ~134MB - MLP contraction reads/writes ~67MB - Add + LN reads/writes ~67MB - Total memory traffic per layer: ~600MB For 12 layers: ~7.2GB of memory traffic. At 3.35TB/s, that's 2.1ms. If compute is 400 GFLOPs at 67 TFLOPS, that's 6ms. Total maybe 8ms. If we fuse attention (avoid QKV and attention output writes), we save ~130MB per layer = 1.5GB total. That's significant. Fusing add+layernorm avoids extra reads/writes: saves ~60MB per occurrence = 120MB per layer = 1.4GB total. So good fusion could reduce memory traffic from ~7.2GB to maybe ~4GB, saving ~1ms on an H100. That's maybe a 10-15% speedup. Not huge, but meaningful. Actually, the main win from C++ is probably avoiding Python overhead and tensor allocation overhead. For 12 layers, Python loops over layers, and each layer creates multiple intermediate tensors. In C++, we can preallocate two buffers and ping-pong between them, avoiding all allocations. This allocation overhead can be significant for small tensors. For 32768 tokens * 1024 hidden = 134MB per large tensor. Creating and destroying these tensors 5 times per layer = 60 tensor allocations per forward pass. PyTorch's caching allocator should handle this, but there can still be overhead. In C++, we can preallocate: - `buffer1` and `buffer2`, each [batch*seq_len, max(hidden_size, intermediate_size, vocab_size)] And reuse them for every layer. This alone could save significant overhead. OK, I think I have a good enough plan. Let me now think about how to handle the exact weight extraction. The weight needs to be transposed so that `hidden_states [B, H] @ weight.t() [H, 3H]` gives `[B, 3H]`. But PyTorch linear layers store weights as `[out_features, in_features]`. So `weight.t()` is `[in_features, out_features]`. Actually, `at::linear(input, weight, bias)` does `input @ weight.t() + bias`. And `at::addmm(bias, input, weight.t())` does the same. So if I want to use `at::addmm`, I can do: ```cpp auto qkv = at::addmm(qkv_b, hidden_states, qkv_w.t()); ``` This computes `qkv_b + hidden_states @ qkv_w.t()`. Since `qkv_w` is `[3H, H]`, `qkv_w.t()` is `[H, 3H]`. Then the result is `[B, 3H]`. Good. Let me write it carefully. First, I need to handle the attention mechanism. For ELECTRA with hidden_size=256, num_attention_heads=4, head_dim=64. In my custom attention kernel, I'll do the following: - Input: QKV tensor of shape [batch*seq_len, 3*hidden_size] - Output: context tensor of shape [batch*seq_len, hidden_size] - Each block handles one sequence - Threads within block handle different heads/query positions Actually, I'll restructure so that each block handles one sequence, and we have multiple phases. Wait, but the QKV tensor is [batch*seq_len, 3*hidden]. For attention, I need to reshape it to [batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim] for Q, K, V. Let me think about the data layout. If QKV is stored as [B*S, 3*H], and for each token, Q, K, V are contiguous: [q_0, q_1, ..., q_H, k_0, ..., k_H, v_0, ..., v_H], then for token at position s in sequence b: - q_ptr = qkv + (b*S + s) * 3H - k_ptr = q_ptr + H - v_ptr = q_ptr + 2H To compute attention for head h and query position i: - q = q_ptr + h * head_dim (but wait, the layout is Q first, then K, then V, each of length H) - Actually, for layout [B*S, 3*H], the Q for head h starts at offset h*head_dim, K starts at H + h*head_dim, V starts at 2H + h*head_dim. But this is per-token. For a query at position i, q is qkv[(b*S + i) * 3H + h*head_dim + d] for d=0..head_dim-1. For key at position j, k is qkv[(b*S + j) * 3H + H + h*head_dim + d]. OK, the indexing is straightforward. For the attention kernel: ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* qkv, float* output, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_heads, int head_dim, int hidden_size ) { int seq_idx = blockIdx.x; if (seq_idx >= batch_size) return; // Each warp handles one head int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; for (int h = warp_id; h < num_heads; h += num_warps) { for (int q_pos = lane_id; q_pos < seq_len; q_pos += 32) { // Compute Q[q_pos] @ K[j] for all j float scores[32]; // assume seq_len <= 32 // Load Q float q[64]; const float* q_ptr = qkv + (seq_idx * seq_len + q_pos) * 3 * hidden_size + h * head_dim; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { q[d] = q_ptr[d]; } float max_score = -1e9f; for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < seq_len; k_pos++) { const float* k_ptr = qkv + (seq_idx * seq_len + k_pos) * 3 * hidden_size + hidden_size + h * head_dim; float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { dot += q[d] * k_ptr[d]; } scores[k_pos] = dot / sqrtf((float)head_dim); max_score = fmaxf(max_score, scores[k_pos]); } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < seq_len; k_pos++) { scores[k_pos] = expf(scores[k_pos] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[k_pos]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Compute attention output float out[64]; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out[d] = 0.0f; } for (int k_pos = 0; k_pos < seq_len; k_pos++) { const float* v_ptr = qkv + (seq_idx * seq_len + k_pos) * 3 * hidden_size + 2 * hidden_size + h * head_dim; float weight = scores[k_pos] * inv_sum; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out[d] += weight * v_ptr[d]; } } // Write output float* out_ptr = output + (seq_idx * seq_len + q_pos) * hidden_size + h * head_dim; for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; d++) { out_ptr[d] = out[d]; } } } } ``` This kernel has each warp handling one head. Within the warp, each thread handles different query positions (every 32nd position). With seq_len=32 and 32 threads in a warp, each warp handles exactly 1 query position per thread, and together they cover all 32 positions for one head. Wait, if `lane_id` ranges from 0 to 31, and `q_pos = lane_id`, then each warp's 32 threads each handle one unique query position from 0 to 31. So one warp processes ALL query positions for one head. And the loop `for (int q_pos = lane_id; q_pos < seq_len; q_pos += 32)` only executes once since lane_id ranges from 0 to 31 and seq_len=32. With `num_warps = num_heads = 4`, we need 4 warps = 128 threads per block. Each block handles one sequence. For 1024 sequences, we launch 1024 blocks of 128 threads each. This seems very clean! And since each warp is independent and handles a full head, there's no need for shared memory or warp-level communication. However, there's a performance issue: threads in a warp are accessing different memory locations for Q (different query positions), but the SAME memory locations for K and V across all threads in the warp. When all 32 threads in a warp read the same K[j] or V[j] address, it broadcasts well. But when they read Q[q_pos], each thread accesses a different address. These accesses are strided by `seq_len * 3 * hidden_size` between query positions. For seq_len=32 and hidden=256, the stride is 32 * 768 * 4 = 98,304 bytes. That's a lot, and with 32 threads accessing locations spaced 98KB apart, there will be 32 separate cache lines loaded. Hmm, but each Q vector is only 64 floats = 256 bytes. So each thread loads 256 bytes, and across the warp they load 32 * 256 = 8KB. With 98KB stride between them, these are in different 128-byte cache lines (or 32-byte segments). So it's 32 memory transactions for Q loading per head. Then for K, each thread reads all 32 keys, so we need 32 keys * 256 bytes = 8KB per thread, times 32 threads = 256KB. But since all threads read the SAME keys in the same order, they can ideally share cache lines. So K and V reads are well-coalesced across the warp in terms of cache utilization. Actually wait, threads in a warp execute together but each thread reads K[0], then K[1], etc. When all threads read K[0] at the same time, since it's the same address, it broadcasts. Good. Same for V. So the key/v value reads are efficient. The main inefficiency is that each thread individually computes the full attention matrix for its query position, resulting in redundant K and V reads from the cache. But since the cache is L1/shared memory, and K/V for a head is 32*64*4 = 8KB, it should fit in L1 cache. So after the first thread reads K[0], it's in cache for the other 31 threads... actually no, in a SIMT warp, all 32 threads execute the `k_ptr[d]` read simultaneously (or near-simultaneously), and since they all read the same address, it broadcasts in one transaction. So this kernel might actually be reasonably efficient! Wait, let me reconsider. In the kernel, for one head, each of the 32 threads in the warp handles one query position. For a given key position j, all 32 threads read k_ptr simultaneously. If k_ptr is the same for all threads, it broadcasts. But k_ptr is NOT the same for all threads! Let me look again: ```cpp const float* k_ptr = qkv + (seq_idx * seq_len + k_pos) * 3 * hidden_size + hidden_size + h * head_dim; ``` Here, `seq_idx` is the same for all threads in the block (same blockIdx.x). `k_pos` is the loop variable, same for all threads at a given point in the loop. `h` is the head, same for all threads in the warp. So k_ptr IS the same for all threads! Yes! Similarly for v_ptr. And q_ptr is different for each thread because of q_pos, but that's expected since each thread handles a different query. So K and V reads are indeed broadcasts across the warp! This means the attention kernel is actually quite efficient given the small size. Great, this kernel design seems solid. Now for the LayerNorm kernel. The standard approach is to have all threads in a block (or warp) cooperate on one token. For our case, with 256 hidden size, I'll have each block process multiple tokens, and within each warp, 256 threads handle one token's layernorm. But a warp only has 32 threads, so we'd need 8 warps = 256 threads for one token. Actually, for hidden=256, we can have each block handle 4 tokens with 256 threads (64 threads per token). Or 2 tokens with 128 threads (64 per token). For a simple approach, let me use one block per token with 256 threads, but we have 32768 tokens, so 32768 blocks. H100 can handle this. Or use 128 threads per block handling 2 tokens each. Let me use 256 threads per block, each block handling 1 token: ```cuda __global__ void layernorm_kernel( const float* input, const float* gamma, const float* beta, float* output, int num_tokens, int hidden_size, float eps ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; if (token_idx >= num_tokens) return; const float* x = input + token_idx * hidden_size; float* y = output + token_idx * hidden_size; __shared__ float mean, var; __shared__ float buf[256]; // Each thread loads one element float val = x[threadIdx.x]; buf[threadIdx.x] = val; // Reduction for mean __syncthreads(); // Parallel reduction in shared memory // For 256 threads: reduce to one value // Can use warp shuffle or loop // ... } ``` Actually, writing an efficient parallel reduction is a bit tedious. For 256 elements, a simple approach: ```cuda float sum = val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 128; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); } if (threadIdx.x % 32 == 0) { atomicAdd(&mean, sum); } __syncthreads(); // But atomicAdd to shared float is tricky... better to use shared memory reduction ``` Let me use a shared memory reduction tree: ```cuda __shared__ float s_sum[256]; s_sum[threadIdx.x] = val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = 128; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { s_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sum[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } mean = s_sum[0] / hidden_size; __syncthreads(); ``` Then compute variance similarly: ```cuda float diff = val - mean; s_sum[threadIdx.x] = diff * diff; __syncthreads(); for (int s = 128; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { s_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sum[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } var = s_sum[0] / hidden_size; __syncthreads(); // Normalize y[threadIdx.x] = (val - mean) / sqrtf(var + eps) * gamma[threadIdx.x] + beta[threadIdx.x]; ``` This is a standard layernorm kernel. For fused add + layernorm: ```cuda __global__ void add_layernorm_kernel( const float* residual, const float* input, const float* gamma, const float* beta, float* output, int num_tokens, int hidden_size, float eps ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; if (token_idx >= num_tokens) return; float val = residual[token_idx * hidden_size + threadIdx.x] + input[token_idx * hidden_size + threadIdx.x]; // ... rest is same as layernorm } ``` For GELU: ```cuda __device__ float gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (x + 0.044715f * x * x * x))); } __global__ void gelu_kernel( const float* input, float* output, int numel ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= numel) return; output[idx] = gelu(input[idx]); } ``` For embeddings, a similar kernel that looks up word, position, type embeddings and applies layernorm: ```cuda __global__ void embeddings_kernel( const int* input_ids, const float* word_embed, const float* pos_embed, const float* type_embed, const float* gamma, const float* beta, float* output, int batch_size, int seq_len, int hidden_size, int vocab_size, float eps ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; if (token_idx >= batch_size * seq_len) return; int input_id = input_ids[token_idx]; float val = word_embed[input_id * hidden_size + threadIdx.x] + pos_embed[(token_idx % seq_len) * hidden_size + threadIdx.x] + type_embed[threadIdx.x]; // token_type_id = 0 // Store, then layernorm __shared__ float s_buf[256]; s_buf[threadIdx.x] = val; __syncthreads(); // Layernorm on s_buf // ... output[token_idx * hidden_size + threadIdx.x] = normalized_val; } ``` Wait, position embeddings depend on position within the sequence, not token_idx. So `(token_idx % seq_len)` gives the correct position for each token in the batch. OK, I think I have a good handle on the kernels. Now let me think about the C++ host code. For the host function, I need to: 1. Create workspace buffers 2. Launch embedding kernel 3. Loop through layers 4. Launch final head kernel or call addmm For workspace buffers: ```cpp auto options = torch::TensorOptions().dtype(torch::kFloat32).device(input_ids.device()); int batch_size = input_ids.size(0); int seq_len = input_ids.size(1); int num_tokens = batch_size * seq_len; auto buffer1 = torch::empty({num_tokens, max_hidden_or_intermediate}, options); auto buffer2 = torch::empty({num_tokens, max_hidden_or_intermediate}, options); ``` But I don't know `max_hidden_or_intermediate` at compile time. In C++, I can compute it as `std::max(std::max(hidden_size, intermediate_size), output_vocab_size)`. Actually, wait. For ELECTRA small, hidden_size=256 and intermediate_size=1024. If the output is vocab_size=30522, then max_size = 30522. That's quite large! Buffer2 would sometimes need to hold [32768, 30522] which is 4GB. That's on the edge of feasibility. If the output is [32768, 1], then max_size = 1024, and buffers are only 134MB each. Let me add a check: if output_size > hidden_size, I might need separate allocation for the final output. Actually, I should just allocate workspace buffers sized for the largest intermediate activation, not including the final output. The final output can be its own tensor. For transformer layers, the largest activation is intermediate_size=1024. So workspace = [32768, 1024] = 134MB. Two such buffers = 268MB. Fine. For the final output, allocate it separately based on the actual output size. Now, one more concern: `at::addmm` in the C++ extension. Is it available and does it work well? Yes. Wait, actually there's a subtlety. The PyTorch C++ API `at::addmm` computes `self @ mat2 + mat1`... no wait, it computes `beta * input + alpha * (mat1 @ mat2)`. Let me check: `at::addmm(const at::Tensor & self, const at::Tensor & mat1, const at::Tensor & mat2, const at::Scalar & beta=1, const at::Scalar & alpha=1)` It computes `beta * self + alpha * (mat1 @ mat2)`. So `at::addmm(bias, input, weight.t())` computes `bias + input @ weight.t()`, which is correct for `linear(input, weight, bias)` if weight is `[out_feat, in_feat]`. Great. For the attention output projection, input is the context [B*S, H] and weight is [H, H]. So `at::addmm(attn_out_bias, context, attn_out_weight.t())`. Wait, but PyTorch typically stores linear weights as `[out_features, in_features]`. So for a `nn.Linear(256, 256)`, weight is `[256, 256]`. Then `at::addmm(bias, input, weight.t())` computes `[B*S, 256] @ [256, 256] + [256]` = correct. For MLP expansion: `nn.Linear(256, 1024)`, weight is `[1024, 256]`. `at::addmm(inter_bias, hidden_states, inter_weight.t())` = `[B*S, 256] @ [256, 1024] + [1024]`. Correct. For MLP contraction: `nn.Linear(1024, 256)`, weight is `[256, 1024]`. `at::addmm(out_bias, intermediate, out_weight.t())` = `[B*S, 1024] @ [1024, 256] + [256]`. Correct. OK. Embeddings + LayerNorm kernel** ```cuda __global__ void embeddings_layernorm_kernel( const int64_t* input_ids, const float* word_embed, const float* pos_embed, const float* type_embed, const float* gamma, const float* beta, float* output, int num_tokens, int seq_len, int hidden_size, int vocab_size, float eps ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; if (token_idx >= num_tokens) return; int seq_pos = token_idx % seq_len; int64_t token_id = input_ids[token_idx]; __shared__ float s_mean, s_var; __shared__ float s_val[256]; float val = word_embed[token_id * hidden_size + threadIdx.x] + pos_embed[seq_pos * hidden_size + threadIdx.x] + type_embed[threadIdx.x]; s_val[threadIdx.x] = val; __syncthreads(); // Reduction for mean // ... } ``` Actually, for hidden_size > 256 or < 256, the shared memory and thread count should adjust. ELECTRA small config: - embedding_size: 128 - hidden_size: 256 In ELECTRA, if `embedding_size != hidden_size`, there's an additional projection layer after embeddings! Wait, this is crucial. Because embedding_size=128. For layernorm on 128 elements, I need 128 threads per block. Each block still does 1 token. Actually, I can make the block size flexible. I'll pass `embedding_size` as an argument, and use `blockDim.x` accordingly. ```cuda __global__ void embeddings_layernorm_kernel( // ... int embedding_size, // ... ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; if (token_idx >= num_tokens) return; // ... float val = word_embed[...] + pos_embed[...] + type_embed[...]; __shared__ float s_val[512]; // max assume 512 s_val[threadIdx.x] = val; __syncthreads(); // reduction with blockDim.x = embedding_size } ``` But CUDA requires shared memory size to be known at compile time, or I can use dynamic shared memory. For simplicity, I'll allocate a reasonably large shared memory and use the actual size dynamically. Or I can use dynamic shared memory: ```cuda extern __shared__ float s_val[]; ``` And pass the shared memory size in the kernel launch: ```cpp embeddings_layernorm_kernel<<<num_tokens, embedding_size, embedding_size * sizeof(float)>>>(...); ``` This is cleaner. For the reduction, with dynamic shared memory: ```cuda extern __shared__ float s_buf[]; float val = ...; s_buf[threadIdx.x] = val; __syncthreads(); // Tree reduction for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_buf[threadIdx.x] += s_buf[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_buf[0] / blockDim.x; __syncthreads(); s_buf[threadIdx.x] = (val - mean) * (val - mean); __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_buf[threadIdx.x] += s_buf[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float var = s_buf[0] / blockDim.x; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); output[token_idx * hidden_size + threadIdx.x] = (val - mean) * inv_std * gamma[threadIdx.x] + beta[threadIdx.x]; ``` Wait, `output[token_idx * hidden_size + threadIdx.x]` assumes output hidden_size equals embedding_size. But that's fine for the embedding kernel, since it outputs the embedding space. OK, this works for any embedding_size up to the max threads per block (1024). One more detail: some ELECTRA implementations use "attention self" with query/key/value projections, but then they might have a `transpose_for_scores` function that changes the shape. Let me verify the actual PyTorch implementation. In `transformers.models.bert.modeling_bert`, `BertSelfAttention` does: ```python mixed_query_layer = self.query(hidden_states) key_layer = self.transpose_for_scores(self.key(hidden_states)) value_layer = self.transpose_for_scores(self.value(hidden_states)) ``` Where `transpose_for_scores` changes from [B, S, H] to [B, num_heads, S, head_dim]. Then attention is computed as: ```python attention_scores = torch.matmul(query_layer, key_layer.transpose(-1, -2)) attention_scores = attention_scores / math.sqrt(head_dim) attention_probs = nn.functional.softmax(attention_scores, dim=-1) context_layer = torch.matmul(attention_probs, value_layer) context_layer = context_layer.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() context_layer = context_layer.view(new_context_layer_shape) ``` This is exactly what my custom attention kernel does! Alright, I think the architecture is correct. Now let me think about numerical precision. My kernels use float32 arithmetic with `expf`, `sqrtf`, etc. This should match PyTorch's FP32 operations closely. One potential discrepancy: `rsqrtf(var + eps)` vs `1.0f / sqrtf(var + eps)`. These should be identical for all practical purposes. Another: my GELU uses `tanhf`. PyTorch's GELU formula is: ``` gelu(x) = 0.5 * x * (1 + tanh(sqrt(2/pi) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3))) ``` where `sqrt(2/pi) ≈ 0.7978845608`. One more opportunity: **fusing the QKV projection with the attention kernel**. If I write a kernel that does the QKV projection internally, then I don't need to write the QKV result to global memory and read it back. For 32768 tokens, QKV projection writes 32768 * 768 * 4 = 96MB. Eliminating this write + read saves 192MB of memory bandwidth per layer. That's 2.3GB total over 12 layers. To implement this, the kernel would need to load the weight matrices [256, 256] for Q, K, V from global memory. Since these are 256KB each, they can fit in L2 cache easily. And since all thread blocks for different sequences need the same weights, they'd be read from L2. Inside the attention kernel, instead of reading QKV from global memory, we compute them on the fly: 1. Load input token: [256] 2. For each head: a. Compute Q = input @ W_Q + b_Q b. Compute K = input @ W_K + b_K c. Compute V = input @ W_V + b_V d. Compute attention using Q, K, V But this means each token's Q, K, V are computed by a single thread (or a few threads). For 256 elements, computing the dot product in one thread would take 256 iterations. That's slow. An alternative: have the warp or block compute QKV cooperatively. But then you need synchronization. Alternatively, just compute QKV using `at::addmm` and accept the memory traffic. The overhead is probably acceptable. Given the complexity of fusing QKV projection into the attention kernel, and the fact that matmul is compute-bound while memory bandwidth is less of a bottleneck for larger tensors, I'll stick with separate `at::addmm` + custom attention kernel. Actually there's one more thing. For `at::addmm` with such small-ish matrices: - [32768, 256] @ [256, 768] = 32768 * 256 * 768 = 6.4B multiply-adds = 12.8 GFLOPs - This is easily compute-bound, taking maybe 0.1-0.2ms on H100 The memory write of 96MB would take ~30 microseconds at 3.35TB/s. So saving it isn't a huge win in absolute terms. OK, what about the intermediate sizes? - MLP expansion: [32768, 256] @ [256, 1024] = 17.1 GFLOPs - MLP contraction: [32768, 1024] @ [1024, 256] = 17.1 GFLOPs These are compute-bound too. The total compute per layer is ~35 GFLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS, that's ~0.5ms per layer, or 6ms for 12 layers. Adding embeddings and head, total maybe 7-8ms. Wait, but 67 TFLOPS is peak theoretical. Realistic sustained might be 30-40 TFLOPS for FP32. So ~12ms for the model. A custom kernel saving 2-3ms from fusion and overhead could be a 20-30% speedup. To be safe, I should try to make the reference and my kernel use the same algorithm. One option: can I also use `at::matmul` for attention in my C++ code? Yes! Instead of a custom attention kernel, I can do attention using ATen ops: ```cpp // QKV = [B*S, 3*H] auto q = qkv.slice(1, 0, H); // [B*S, H] auto k = qkv.slice(1, H, 2*H); // [B*S, H] auto v = qkv.slice(1, 2*H, 3*H); // [B*S, H] // Reshape to [B, S, num_heads, head_dim] and permute to [B, num_heads, S, head_dim] auto q_4d = q.reshape({B, S, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); // [B, 4, 32, 64] auto k_4d = k.reshape({B, S, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); auto v_4d = v.reshape({B, S, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); // Attention scores: [B, heads, S, S] auto scores = at::matmul(q_4d, k_4d.transpose(-1, -2)); scores = scores / sqrt(head_dim); // Softmax auto attn_probs = at::softmax(scores, -1); // Context auto context = at::matmul(attn_probs, v_4d); // Reshape back to [B*S, H] context = context.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).reshape({B*S, H}); ``` This uses ATen ops for attention, which means it uses the same TF32 settings as the reference. And it might be just as fast for small attention, since ATen dispatches to optimized CUDA kernels. If I use ATen for attention too, then the only custom kernels are: 1. `embeddings_layernorm_kernel` 2. `add_layernorm_kernel` That's fewer custom kernels but more reliable correctness. And `add_layernorm` is a clear win over separate add + layernorm. Actually, `at::softmax` and `at::matmul` for attention on small tensors might be reasonable. But for seq_len=32, `at::matmul` on [B, 4, 32, 64] @ [B, 4, 64, 32] produces [B, 4, 32, 32]. This is a batched matmul. cuBLAS handles this efficiently. And the second matmul [B, 4, 32, 32] @ [B, 4, 32, 64] -> [B, 4, 32, 64] is also efficient. Actually, PyTorch's `at::matmul` for these small batched matrices might even use Tensor Cores if the dimensions align well. 32x32 is small but 64 might trigger some optimizations. This ATen-based attention approach is attractive because it's much simpler to implement and more likely to match numerically. Custom kernel: embeddings + layernorm(128) -> [B*S, 128] 2. `at::addmm`: projection [B*S, 128] @ [128, 256] -> [B*S, 256] 3. Here's my final structured code: ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> // Kernel 1: Embeddings + LayerNorm __global__ void embeddings_layernorm_kernel( const int64_t* input_ids, const float* word_embed, const float* pos_embed, const float* type_embed, const float* gamma, const float* beta, float* output, int num_tokens, int seq_len, int embedding_size, int vocab_size, float eps ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; if (token_idx >= num_tokens) return; int seq_pos = token_idx % seq_len; int64_t token_id = input_ids[token_idx]; extern __shared__ float s_buf[]; float val = 0.0f; if (token_id >= 0 && token_id < vocab_size) { val = word_embed[token_id * embedding_size + threadIdx.x]; } val += pos_embed[seq_pos * embedding_size + threadIdx.x]; val += type_embed[threadIdx.x]; s_buf[threadIdx.x] = val; __syncthreads(); // Compute mean for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_buf[threadIdx.x] += s_buf[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_buf[0] / embedding_size; // Compute variance s_buf[threadIdx.x] = (val - mean) * (val - mean); __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_buf[threadIdx.x] += s_buf[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float var = s_buf[0] / embedding_size; output[token_idx * embedding_size + threadIdx.x] = (val - mean) * rsqrtf(var + eps) * gamma[threadIdx.x] + beta[threadIdx.x]; } // Kernel 2: Add residual + LayerNorm __global__ void add_layernorm_kernel( const float* residual, const float* input, const float* gamma, const float* beta, float* output, int num_tokens, int hidden_size, float eps ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; if (token_idx >= num_tokens) return; extern __shared__ float s_buf[]; float val = residual[token_idx * hidden_size + threadIdx.x] + input[token_idx * hidden_size + threadIdx.x]; s_buf[threadIdx.x] = val; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_buf[threadIdx.x] += s_buf[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_buf[0] / hidden_size; s_buf[threadIdx.x] = (val - mean) * (val - mean); __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_buf[threadIdx.x] += s_buf[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float var = s_buf[0] / hidden_size; output[token_idx * hidden_size + threadIdx.x] = (val - mean) * rsqrtf(var + eps) * gamma[threadIdx.x] + beta[threadIdx.x]; } // Host function torch::Tensor electra_forward( torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor word_embed, torch::Tensor pos_embed, torch::Tensor type_embed, torch::Tensor embed_ln_w, torch::Tensor embed_ln_b, torch::Tensor qkv_weights, torch::Tensor qkv_biases, torch::Tensor attn_out_weights, torch::Tensor attn_out_biases, torch::Tensor attn_ln_weights, torch::Tensor attn_ln_biases, torch::Tensor inter_weights, torch::Tensor inter_biases, torch::Tensor out_weights, torch::Tensor out_biases, torch::Tensor out_ln_weights, torch::Tensor out_ln_biases, torch::Tensor proj_weight, torch::Tensor proj_bias, torch::Tensor head1_weight, torch::Tensor head1_bias, torch::Tensor head2_weight, torch::Tensor head2_bias ) { // ... implementation ... } ''' ``` Then the implementation of `electra_forward`: ```cpp torch::Tensor electra_forward(...) { auto options = input_ids.options().dtype(torch::kFloat32); // [V, E] int intermediate_size = inter_weights.size(1); // [L, I, H] int num_heads = hidden_size / 64; // Or can infer from weight shapes? OK, let me refine the implementation of the host function: ```cpp torch::Tensor electra_forward(...) { // Infer params int batch_size = input_ids.size(0); int seq_len = input_ids.size(1); int num_tokens = batch_size * seq_len; int num_layers = qkv_weights.size(0); int embedding_size = word_embed.size(1); int hidden_size = qkv_weights.size(2); int intermediate_size = inter_weights.size(1); int head_dim = hidden_size / num_heads; float eps = layer_norm_eps; auto options = word_embed.options(); // Embedding kernel auto embed_output = torch::empty({num_tokens, embedding_size}, options); embeddings_layernorm_kernel<<<num_tokens, embedding_size, embedding_size * sizeof(float)>>>( input_ids.data_ptr<int64_t>(), word_embed.data_ptr<float>(), pos_embed.data_ptr<float>(), type_embed.data_ptr<float>(), embed_ln_w.data_ptr<float>(), embed_ln_b.data_ptr<float>(), embed_output.data_ptr<float>(), num_tokens, seq_len, embedding_size, vocab_size, eps ); // Projection if needed torch::Tensor hidden_states; if (proj_weight.numel() > 0) { hidden_states = at::addmm(proj_bias, embed_output, proj_weight.t()); } else { hidden_states = embed_output; } // Pre-allocate workspace buffers auto buf_a = torch::empty({num_tokens, hidden_size}, options); auto buf_b = torch::empty({num_tokens, hidden_size}, options); // Copy initial hidden_states to buf_a buf_a.copy_(hidden_states); // Layer loop for (int l = 0; l < num_layers; l++) { // QKV projection auto qkv = at::addmm(qkv_biases[l], buf_a, qkv_weights[l].t()); // Attention using ATen auto q = qkv.slice(1, 0, hidden_size); auto k = qkv.slice(1, hidden_size, 2 * hidden_size); auto v = qkv.slice(1, 2 * hidden_size, 3 * hidden_size); auto q_4d = q.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); auto k_4d = k.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); auto v_4d = v.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); auto scores = at::matmul(q_4d, k_4d.transpose(-1, -2)); scores = scores / sqrt((float)head_dim); auto attn_probs = at::softmax(scores, -1); auto context = at::matmul(attn_probs, v_4d); context = context.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).reshape({num_tokens, hidden_size}); // Output projection auto attn_out = at::addmm(attn_out_biases[l], context, attn_out_weights[l].t()); // Add residual + LN -> buf_b add_layernorm_kernel<<<num_tokens, hidden_size, hidden_size * sizeof(float)>>>( buf_a.data_ptr<float>(), attn_out.data_ptr<float>(), attn_ln_weights[l].data_ptr<float>(), attn_ln_biases[l].data_ptr<float>(), buf_b.data_ptr<float>(), num_tokens, hidden_size, eps ); // MLP expansion auto inter = at::addmm(inter_biases[l], buf_b, inter_weights[l].t()); // GELU inter = at::gelu(inter); // MLP contraction auto out = at::addmm(out_biases[l], inter, out_weights[l].t()); // Add residual + LN -> buf_a add_layernorm_kernel<<<num_tokens, hidden_size, hidden_size * sizeof(float)>>>( buf_b.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), out_ln_weights[l].data_ptr<float>(), out_ln_biases[l].data_ptr<float>(), buf_a.data_ptr<float>(), num_tokens, hidden_size, eps ); } // Head torch::Tensor logits; if (head2_weight.numel() > 0) { auto temp = at::addmm(head1_bias, buf_a, head1_weight.t()); temp = at::gelu(temp); logits = at::addmm(head2_bias, temp, head2_weight.t()); } else { logits = at::addmm(head1_bias, buf_a, head1_weight.t()); } // Reshape logits to [batch_size, seq_len, -1] or [batch_size, seq_len] if (logits.size(1) == 1) { logits = logits.reshape({batch_size, seq_len}); } else { logits = logits.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, logits.size(1)}); } return logits; } ``` Hmm, this looks correct for the most part. gamma[threadIdx.x] + beta[threadIdx.x];\n}\n\n// Kernel: fused residual add + LayerNorm\n__global__ void add_layernorm_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ residual,\n const float* __restrict__ __syncthreads();\n }\n float mean = s_buf[0] / hidden_size;\n\n s_buf[threadIdx.x] = (val - mean) * (val - mean);\n __syncthreads();\n for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) {\n 3});\n auto v_4d = v.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3});\n\n auto scores = at::matmul(q_4d, k_4d.transpose(-1, -2));\n scores = scores / sqrtf((float)head_dim);\n\n if (use_causal_mask) {\n scores = scores.masked_fill(causal_mask.to(torch::kBool), -1e9f);\n }\n\n auto attn_probs = at::softmax(scores, -1);\n auto context = import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline
from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig

_CUDA_SOURCE = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <math.h>

// Kernel: fused embedding lookup + LayerNorm
__global__ void embeddings_layernorm_kernel(
    const int64_t* __restrict__ input_ids,
    const float*   __restrict__ word_embed,
    const float*   __restrict__ pos_embed,
    const float*   __restrict__ type_embed,
    const float*   __restrict__ gamma,
    const float*   __restrict__ beta,
    float*         __restrict__ output,
    int num_tokens,
    int seq_len,
    int embedding_size,
    int vocab_size,
    float eps)
{
    int token_idx = blockIdx.x;
    if (token_idx >= num_tokens) return;

    int seq_pos  = token_idx % seq_len;
    int64_t tok  = input_ids[token_idx];

    extern __shared__ float s_buf[];

    float val = 0.0f;
    if (tok >= 0 && tok < vocab_size) {
        val = word_embed[tok * embedding_size + threadIdx.x];
    }
    val += pos_embed[seq_pos * embedding_size + threadIdx.x];
    val += type_embed[threadIdx.x];

    s_buf[threadIdx.x] = val;
    __syncthreads();

    for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) {
        if (threadIdx.x < stride) {
            s_buf[threadIdx.x] += s_buf[threadIdx.x + stride];
        }
        __syncthreads();
    }
    float mean = s_buf[0] / embedding_size;

    s_buf[threadIdx.x] = (val - mean) * (val - mean);
    __syncthreads();
    for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) {
        if (threadIdx.x < stride) {
            s_buf[threadIdx.x] += s_buf[threadIdx.x + stride];
        }
        __syncthreads();
    }
    float var = s_buf[0] / embedding_size;

    output[token_idx * embedding_size + threadIdx.x] =
        (val - mean) * rsqrtf(var + eps) * gamma[threadIdx.x] + beta[threadIdx.x];
}

// Kernel: fused residual add + LayerNorm
__global__ void add_layernorm_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ residual,
    const float* __restrict__ input,
    const float* __restrict__ gamma,
    const float* __restrict__ beta,
    float*       __restrict__ output,
    int num_tokens,
    int hidden_size,
    float eps)
{
    int token_idx = blockIdx.x;
    if (token_idx >= num_tokens) return;

    extern __shared__ float s_buf[];

    float val = residual[token_idx * hidden_size + threadIdx.x]
              + input[token_idx * hidden_size + threadIdx.x];

    s_buf[threadIdx.x] = val;
    __syncthreads();

    for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) {
        if (threadIdx.x < stride) {
            s_buf[threadIdx.x] += s_buf[threadIdx.x + stride];
        }
        __syncthreads();
    }
    float mean = s_buf[0] / hidden_size;

    s_buf[threadIdx.x] = (val - mean) * (val - mean);
    __syncthreads();
    for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) {
        if (threadIdx.x < stride) {
            s_buf[threadIdx.x] += s_buf[threadIdx.x + stride];
        }
        __syncthreads();
    }
    float var = s_buf[0] / hidden_size;

    output[token_idx * hidden_size + threadIdx.x] =
        (val - mean) * rsqrtf(var + eps) * gamma[threadIdx.x] + beta[threadIdx.x];
}

// Host-side forward function
torch::Tensor electra_forward(
    torch::Tensor input_ids,
    torch::Tensor word_embed,
    torch::Tensor pos_embed,
    torch::Tensor type_embed,
    torch::Tensor embed_ln_w,
    torch::Tensor embed_ln_b,
    torch::Tensor qkv_weights,
    torch::Tensor qkv_biases,
    torch::Tensor attn_out_weights,
    torch::Tensor attn_out_biases,
    torch::Tensor attn_ln_weights,
    torch::Tensor attn_ln_biases,
    torch::Tensor inter_weights,
    torch::Tensor inter_biases,
    torch::Tensor out_weights,
    torch::Tensor out_biases,
    torch::Tensor out_ln_weights,
    torch::Tensor out_ln_biases,
    torch::Tensor proj_weight,
    torch::Tensor proj_bias,
    torch::Tensor head1_weight,
    torch::Tensor head1_bias,
    torch::Tensor head2_weight,
    torch::Tensor head2_bias,
    int num_heads,
    float layer_norm_eps,
    bool use_causal_mask)
{
    auto options = word_embed.options();
    int batch_size = (int)input_ids.size(0);
    int seq_len    = (int)input_ids.size(1);
    int num_tokens = batch_size * seq_len;
    int num_layers = (int)qkv_weights.size(0);
    int embedding_size = (int)word_embed.size(1);
    int hidden_size    = (int)qkv_weights.size(2);
    int intermediate_size = (int)inter_weights.size(1);
    int head_dim = hidden_size / num_heads;
    float eps = layer_norm_eps;
    int vocab_size = (int)word_embed.size(0);

    auto embed_out = torch::empty({num_tokens, embedding_size}, options);
    embeddings_layernorm_kernel<<<num_tokens, embedding_size, embedding_size * sizeof(float)>>>(
        input_ids.data_ptr<int64_t>(),
        word_embed.data_ptr<float>(),
        pos_embed.data_ptr<float>(),
        type_embed.data_ptr<float>(),
        embed_ln_w.data_ptr<float>(),
        embed_ln_b.data_ptr<float>(),
        embed_out.data_ptr<float>(),
        num_tokens, seq_len, embedding_size, vocab_size, eps);

    torch::Tensor hidden;
    if (proj_weight.numel() > 0) {
        hidden = at::addmm(proj_bias, embed_out, proj_weight.t());
    } else {
        hidden = embed_out;
    }

    auto buf_a = torch::empty({num_tokens, hidden_size}, options);
    auto buf_b = torch::empty({num_tokens, hidden_size}, options);
    buf_a.copy_(hidden);

    torch::Tensor causal_mask;
    if (use_causal_mask) {
        auto mask_2d = at::triu(at::ones({seq_len, seq_len}, options), 1);
        causal_mask = mask_2d.unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(0);
    }

    for (int l = 0; l < num_layers; ++l) {
        auto q = at::addmm(qkv_biases[l].slice(0, 0, hidden_size),
                           buf_a,
                           qkv_weights[l].slice(0, 0, hidden_size).t());
        auto k = at::addmm(qkv_biases[l].slice(0, hidden_size, 2 * hidden_size),
                           buf_a,
                           qkv_weights[l].slice(0, hidden_size, 2 * hidden_size).t());
        auto v = at::addmm(qkv_biases[l].slice(0, 2 * hidden_size, 3 * hidden_size),
                           buf_a,
                           qkv_weights[l].slice(0, 2 * hidden_size, 3 * hidden_size).t());

        auto q_4d = q.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3});
        auto k_4d = k.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3});
        auto v_4d = v.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3});

        auto scores = at::matmul(q_4d, k_4d.transpose(-1, -2));
        scores = scores / sqrtf((float)head_dim);

        if (use_causal_mask) {
            scores = scores.masked_fill(causal_mask.to(torch::kBool), -1e9f);
        }

        auto attn_probs = at::softmax(scores, -1);
        auto context = at::matmul(attn_probs, v_4d);
        context = context.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).reshape({num_tokens, hidden_size});

        auto attn_out = at::addmm(attn_out_biases[l], context, attn_out_weights[l].t());

        add_layernorm_kernel<<<num_tokens, hidden_size, hidden_size * sizeof(float)>>>(
            buf_a.data_ptr<float>(),
            attn_out.data_ptr<float>(),
            attn_ln_weights[l].data_ptr<float>(),
            attn_ln_biases[l].data_ptr<float>(),
            buf_b.data_ptr<float>(),
            num_tokens, hidden_size, eps);

        auto inter = at::addmm(inter_biases[l], buf_b, inter_weights[l].t());
        inter = at::gelu(inter);
        auto out = at::addmm(out_biases[l], inter, out_weights[l].t());

        add_layernorm_kernel<<<num_tokens, hidden_size, hidden_size * sizeof(float)>>>(
            buf_b.data_ptr<float>(),
            out.data_ptr<float>(),
            out_ln_weights[l].data_ptr<float>(),
            out_ln_biases[l].data_ptr<float>(),
            buf_a.data_ptr<float>(),
            num_tokens, hidden_size, eps);
    }

    torch::Tensor logits;
    if (head2_weight.numel() > 0) {
        auto tmp = at::addmm(head1_bias, buf_a, head1_weight.t());
        tmp = at::gelu(tmp);
        logits = at::addmm(head2_bias, tmp, head2_weight.t());
    } else {
        logits = at::addmm(head1_bias, buf_a, head1_weight.t());
    }

    if (logits.size(1) == 1) {
        logits = logits.reshape({batch_size, seq_len});
    } else {
        logits = logits.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, logits.size(1)});
    }
    return logits;
}
'''

_CPP_SOURCE = r'''
torch::Tensor electra_forward(
    torch::Tensor input_ids,
    torch::Tensor word_embed,
    torch::Tensor pos_embed,
    torch::Tensor type_embed,
    torch::Tensor embed_ln_w,
    torch::Tensor embed_ln_b,
    torch::Tensor qkv_weights,
    torch::Tensor qkv_biases,
    torch::Tensor attn_out_weights,
    torch::Tensor attn_out_biases,
    torch::Tensor attn_ln_weights,
    torch::Tensor attn_ln_biases,
    torch::Tensor inter_weights,
    torch::Tensor inter_biases,
    torch::Tensor out_weights,
    torch::Tensor out_biases,
    torch::Tensor out_ln_weights,
    torch::Tensor out_ln_biases,
    torch::Tensor proj_weight,
    torch::Tensor proj_bias,
    torch::Tensor head1_weight,
    torch::Tensor head1_bias,
    torch::Tensor head2_weight,
    torch::Tensor head2_bias,
    int num_heads,
    float layer_norm_eps,
    bool use_causal_mask);
'''

electra_cuda = load_inline(
    name='electra_cuda_forward',
    cpp_sources=_CPP_SOURCE,
    cuda_sources=_CUDA_SOURCE,
    functions=['electra_forward'],
    extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'],
    extra_ldflags=['-static-libstdc++'],
    verbose=False,
)


class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config

        self._ref_model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config)
        self._ref_model.eval()

        sd = dict(self._ref_model.named_parameters())

        layer_nums = set()
        for k in sd:
            if 'encoder.layer.' in k:
                parts = k.split('layer.')
                if len(parts) > 1:
                    n = parts[1].split('.')[0]
                    if n.isdigit():
                        layer_nums.add(int(n))
        num_layers = max(layer_nums) + 1 if layer_nums else 0
        self.num_layers = num_layers

        def get_param(*patterns):
            for k, v in sd.items():
                if all(p in k for p in patterns):
                    return v.detach().contiguous()
            return None

        word_embed = get_param('word_embeddings', 'weight')
        pos_embed  = get_param('position_embeddings', 'weight')
        type_embed = get_param('token_type_embeddings', 'weight')
        embed_ln_w = get_param('embeddings', 'LayerNorm', 'weight')
        embed_ln_b = get_param('embeddings', 'LayerNorm', 'bias')

        proj_w = get_param('embeddings_project', 'weight')
        proj_b = get_param('embeddings_project', 'bias')

        qkv_ws, qkv_bs = [], []
        attn_ow, attn_ob = [], []
        attn_lnw, attn_lnb = [], []
        inter_ws, inter_bs = [], []
        out_ws, out_bs = [], []
        out_lnw, out_lnb = [], []

        for i in range(num_layers):
            qw = get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'query', 'weight')
            qb = get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'query', 'bias')
            kw = get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'key', 'weight')
            kb = get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'key', 'bias')
            vw = get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'value', 'weight')
            vb = get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'value', 'bias')
            qkv_w = torch.cat([qw, kw, vw], dim=0)
            qkv_b = torch.cat([qb, kb, vb], dim=0)
            qkv_ws.append(qkv_w)
            qkv_bs.append(qkv_b)

            attn_ow.append(get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'attention.output.dense', 'weight'))
            attn_ob.append(get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'attention.output.dense', 'bias'))
            attn_lnw.append(get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'attention.output.LayerNorm', 'weight'))
            attn_lnb.append(get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'attention.output.LayerNorm', 'bias'))

            inter_ws.append(get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'intermediate.dense', 'weight'))
            inter_bs.append(get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'intermediate.dense', 'bias'))
            out_ws.append(get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'output.dense', 'weight'))
            out_bs.append(get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'output.dense', 'bias'))
            out_lnw.append(get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'output.LayerNorm', 'weight'))
            out_lnb.append(get_param(f'layer.{i}.', 'output.LayerNorm', 'bias'))

        head_candidates = []
        for k, v in sd.items():
            if 'weight' not in k:
                continue
            if 'embeddings' in k or 'encoder.layer' in k:
                continue
            if len(v.shape) == 2:
                head_candidates.append((k, v))
        head_candidates.sort(key=lambda x: x[0])

        head1_w = head1_b = head2_w = head2_b = None
        if len(head_candidates) >= 2:
            head1_w = head_candidates[0][1].detach().contiguous()
            key1 = head_candidates[0][0].replace('.weight', '.bias')
            head1_b = sd.get(key1)
            if head1_b is not None:
                head1_b = head1_b.detach().contiguous()
            head2_w = head_candidates[1][1].detach().contiguous()
            key2 = head_candidates[1][0].replace('.weight', '.bias')
            head2_b = sd.get(key2)
            if head2_b is not None:
                head2_b = head2_b.detach().contiguous()
        elif len(head_candidates) == 1:
            head1_w = head_candidates[0][1].detach().contiguous()
            key1 = head_candidates[0][0].replace('.weight', '.bias')
            head1_b = sd.get(key1)
            if head1_b is not None:
                head1_b = head1_b.detach().contiguous()
            head2_w = torch.empty(0)
            head2_b = torch.empty(0)
        else:
            head1_w = torch.empty(0)
            head1_b = torch.empty(0)
            head2_w = torch.empty(0)
            head2_b = torch.empty(0)

        device = next(self._ref_model.parameters()).device

        def to_device(t):
            if t is None:
                return torch.empty(0, device=device)
            return t.to(device)

        self.register_buffer('word_embed', to_device(word_embed))
        self.register_buffer('pos_embed', to_device(pos_embed))
        self.register_buffer('type_embed', to_device(type_embed))
        self.register_buffer('embed_ln_w', to_device(embed_ln_w))
        self.register_buffer('embed_ln_b', to_device(embed_ln_b))
        self.register_buffer('proj_weight', to_device(proj_w))
        self.register_buffer('proj_bias', to_device(proj_b))

        self.register_buffer('qkv_weights', to_device(torch.stack(qkv_ws)))
        self.register_buffer('qkv_biases', to_device(torch.stack(qkv_bs)))
        self.register_buffer('attn_out_weights', to_device(torch.stack(attn_ow)))
        self.register_buffer('attn_out_biases', to_device(torch.stack(attn_ob)))
        self.register_buffer('attn_ln_weights', to_device(torch.stack(attn_lnw)))
        self.register_buffer('attn_ln_biases', to_device(torch.stack(attn_lnb)))
        self.register_buffer('inter_weights', to_device(torch.stack(inter_ws)))
        self.register_buffer('inter_biases', to_device(torch.stack(inter_bs)))
        self.register_buffer('out_weights', to_device(torch.stack(out_ws)))
        self.register_buffer('out_biases', to_device(torch.stack(out_bs)))
        self.register_buffer('out_ln_weights', to_device(torch.stack(out_lnw)))
        self.register_buffer('out_ln_biases', to_device(torch.stack(out_lnb)))

        self.register_buffer('head1_weight', to_device(head1_w))
        self.register_buffer('head1_bias', to_device(head1_b))
        self.register_buffer('head2_weight', to_device(head2_w))
        self.register_buffer('head2_bias', to_device(head2_b))

        self.num_heads = config.num_attention_heads
        self.layer_norm_eps = config.layer_norm_eps
        cls_name = type(self._ref_model).__name__.lower()
        self.use_causal_mask = 'causal' in cls_name or 'lmhead' in cls_name or 'clm' in cls_name

    def forward(self, x):
        x = x.to(torch.int64)
        if x.device != self.word_embed.device:
            x = x.to(self.word_embed.device)
        return electra_cuda.electra_forward(
            x,
            self.word_embed,
            self.pos_embed,
            self.type_embed,
            self.embed_ln_w,
            self.embed_ln_b,
            self.qkv_weights,
            self.qkv_biases,
            self.attn_out_weights,
            self.attn_out_biases,
            self.attn_ln_weights,
            self.attn_ln_biases,
            self.inter_weights,
            self.inter_biases,
            self.out_weights,
            self.out_biases,
            self.out_ln_weights,
            self.out_ln_biases,
            self.proj_weight,
            self.proj_bias,
            self.head1_weight,
            self.head1_bias,
            self.head2_weight,
            self.head2_bias,
            self.num_heads,
            self.layer_norm_eps,
            self.use_causal_mask,
        )
